### Foreign relations

Main article: [Foreign relations of Lithuania](/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Lithuania "Foreign relations of Lithuania")

Lithuania became a member of the United Nations on 18 September 1991, and is a signatory to a number of its organizations and other international agreements. It is also a member of the [European Union](/wiki/European_Union "European Union"), the [Council of Europe](/wiki/Council_of_Europe "Council of Europe"), [Organization for Security and Cooperation in Europe](/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Cooperation_in_Europe "Organization for Security and Cooperation in Europe"), as well as [NATO](/wiki/NATO "NATO") and its adjunct North Atlantic Coordinating Council. Lithuania gained membership in the [World Trade Organization](/wiki/World_Trade_Organization "World Trade Organization") on 31 May 2001, and joined the [OECD](/wiki/OECD "OECD") on 5 July 2018,[[151]](#cite_note-Lithuania's_accession_to_the_OECD-155) while also seeking membership in other Western organizations.

Lithuania has established diplomatic relations with 149 countries.[[152]](#cite_note-156)

In 2011, Lithuania hosted the [Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe](/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe "Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe") Ministerial Council Meeting. During the second half of 2013, Lithuania assumed the role of the [presidency of the European Union](/wiki/Presidency_of_the_Council_of_the_European_Union "Presidency of the Council of the European Union").

Lithuania is also active in developing cooperation among northern European countries. It is a member of the interparliamentary [Baltic Assembly](/wiki/Baltic_Assembly "Baltic Assembly"), the intergovernmental [Baltic Council of Ministers](/wiki/Baltic_Council_of_Ministers "Baltic Council of Ministers") and the [Council of the Baltic Sea States](/wiki/Council_of_the_Baltic_Sea_States "Council of the Baltic Sea States").

Lithuania also cooperates with Nordic and the two other Baltic countries through the [Nordic-Baltic Eight](/wiki/Nordic-Baltic_Eight "Nordic-Baltic Eight") format. A similar format, NB6, unites Nordic and Baltic members of EU. NB6's focus is to discuss and agree on positions before presenting them to the Council of the European Union and at the meetings of EU foreign affairs ministers.

The [Council of the Baltic Sea States](/wiki/Council_of_the_Baltic_Sea_States "Council of the Baltic Sea States") (CBSS) was established in [Copenhagen](/wiki/Copenhagen "Copenhagen") in 1992 as an informal regional political forum. Its main aim is to promote integration and to close contacts between the region's countries. The members of CBSS are [Iceland](/wiki/Iceland "Iceland"), [Sweden](/wiki/Sweden "Sweden"), [Denmark](/wiki/Denmark "Denmark"), [Norway](/wiki/Norway "Norway"), [Finland](/wiki/Finland "Finland"), [Germany](/wiki/Germany "Germany"), Lithuania, [Latvia](/wiki/Latvia "Latvia"), [Estonia](/wiki/Estonia "Estonia"), [Poland](/wiki/Poland "Poland"), [Russia](/wiki/Russia "Russia"), and the European Commission. Its observer states are [Belarus](/wiki/Belarus "Belarus"), [France](/wiki/France "France"), [Italy](/wiki/Italy "Italy"), [Netherlands](/wiki/Netherlands "Netherlands"), [Romania](/wiki/Romania "Romania"), [Slovakia](/wiki/Slovakia "Slovakia"), [Spain](/wiki/Spain "Spain"), the [United States](/wiki/United_States "United States"), the [United Kingdom](/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom"), and [Ukraine](/wiki/Ukraine "Ukraine").

The [Nordic Council of Ministers](/wiki/Nordic_Council "Nordic Council") and Lithuania engage in political cooperation to attain mutual goals and to determine new trends and possibilities for joint cooperation. The council's information office aims to disseminate [Nordic](/wiki/Nordic_countries "Nordic countries") concepts and to demonstrate and promote Nordic cooperation.

Lithuania, together with the five Nordic countries and the two other Baltic countries, is a member of the [Nordic Investment Bank (NIB)](/wiki/Nordic_Investment_Bank "Nordic Investment Bank") and cooperates in its NORDPLUS programme, which is committed to education.

The Baltic Development Forum (BDF) is an independent nonprofit organization that unites large companies, cities, business associations and institutions in the Baltic Sea region. In 2010 the BDF's 12th summit was held in Vilnius.[[153]](#cite_note-157)

[Poland](/wiki/Poland "Poland") was highly supportive of Lithuanian independence, despite Lithuania's [discriminatory treatment](/wiki/Lithuanization "Lithuanization") of its [Polish minority](/wiki/Poles_in_Lithuania "Poles in Lithuania").[[154]](#cite_note-158)[[155]](#cite_note-159) The former [Solidarity](/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union) "Solidarity (Polish trade union)") leader and Polish President [Lech Wałęsa](/wiki/Lech_Wa%C5%82%C4%99sa "Lech Wałęsa") criticised the government of Lithuania over discrimination against the Polish minority and rejected Lithuania's [Order of Vytautas the Great](/wiki/Order_of_Vytautas_the_Great "Order of Vytautas the Great").[[156]](#cite_note-160) Lithuania maintains greatly warm mutual relations with [Georgia](/wiki/Georgia_(country) "Georgia (country)") and strongly supports its European Union and NATO aspirations.[[157]](#cite_note-161)[[158]](#cite_note-162)[[159]](#cite_note-163) During the [Russo-Georgian War](/wiki/Russo-Georgian_War "Russo-Georgian War") in 2008, when the Russian troops were occupying the territory of Georgia and approaching towards the Georgian capital [Tbilisi](/wiki/Tbilisi "Tbilisi"), President [Valdas Adamkus](/wiki/Valdas_Adamkus "Valdas Adamkus"), together with the Polish and Ukrainian presidents, went to Tbilisi by answering to the Georgians request of the international assistance.[[160]](#cite_note-164)[[161]](#cite_note-165) Shortly, Lithuanians and the [Lithuanian Catholic Church](/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_Lithuania "Catholic Church in Lithuania") also began collecting financial support for the war victims.[[162]](#cite_note-166)[[163]](#cite_note-167)

In 2004–2009, [Dalia Grybauskaitė](/wiki/Dalia_Grybauskait%C4%97 "Dalia Grybauskaitė") served as [European Commissioner for Financial Programming and the Budget](/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Financial_Programming_and_the_Budget "European Commissioner for Financial Programming and the Budget") within the [José Manuel Barroso](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Manuel_Barroso "José Manuel Barroso")-led Commission.[[164]](#cite_note-168)[[165]](#cite_note-169)

In 2013, Lithuania was elected to the [United Nations Security Council](/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council "United Nations Security Council") for a two-year term,[[166]](#cite_note-170) becoming the first [Baltic](/wiki/Baltic_states "Baltic states") country elected to this post. During its membership, Lithuania actively supported [Ukraine](/wiki/Ukraine "Ukraine") and often condemned [Russia](/wiki/Russia "Russia") for the [war in Ukraine](/wiki/Russo-Ukrainian_War "Russo-Ukrainian War"), immediately earning vast Ukrainians esteem.[[167]](#cite_note-171)[[168]](#cite_note-172) As the [war in Donbas](/wiki/War_in_Donbas_(2014%E2%80%932022) "War in Donbas (2014–2022)") progressed, President Dalia Grybauskaitė has compared the Russian President [Vladimir Putin](/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") to [Josef Stalin](/wiki/Josef_Stalin "Josef Stalin") and to [Adolf Hitler](/wiki/Adolf_Hitler "Adolf Hitler"), she has also called Russia a "terrorist state".[[169]](#cite_note-173)

In 2018 Lithuania, along with Latvia and Estonia were awarded the [Peace of Westphalia Prize](/w/index.php?title=Peace_of_Westphalia_Prize&action=edit&redlink=1 "Peace of Westphalia Prize (page does not exist)") [[de](https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internationaler_Preis_des_Westf%C3%A4lischen_Friedens "de:Internationaler Preis des Westfälischen Friedens")] – for their exceptional model of democratic development and contribution to peace in the continent.[[170]](#cite_note-174) In 2019 Lithuania condemned the [Turkish offensive into north-eastern Syria](/wiki/2019_Turkish_offensive_into_north-eastern_Syria "2019 Turkish offensive into north-eastern Syria").[[171]](#cite_note-175) In December 2021, Lithuania reported that in an escalation of the diplomatic spat with [China](/wiki/China "China") over its [relations with Taiwan](/wiki/Lithuania%E2%80%93Taiwan_relations "Lithuania–Taiwan relations"),[[172]](#cite_note-176) China had stopped all imports from Lithuania.[[173]](#cite_note-177) According to Lithuanian intelligence agencies, in 2023 there was an increase in Chinese intelligence activity against Lithuania, including cyberespionage and increased focus on Lithuania's internal affairs and foreign policy.[[174]](#cite_note-178)

The 2023 [NATO summit](/wiki/NATO_summit "NATO summit") was held in the Lithuanian capital [Vilnius](/wiki/Vilnius "Vilnius").[[175]](#cite_note-179)

### Military

Main article: [Lithuanian Armed Forces](/wiki/Lithuanian_Armed_Forces "Lithuanian Armed Forces")

The Lithuanian Armed Forces is the name for the unified armed forces of [Lithuanian Land Force](/wiki/Lithuanian_Land_Force "Lithuanian Land Force"), [Lithuanian Air Force](/wiki/Lithuanian_Air_Force "Lithuanian Air Force"), [Lithuanian Naval Force](/wiki/Lithuanian_Naval_Force "Lithuanian Naval Force"), [Lithuanian Special Operations Force](/wiki/Lithuanian_Special_Operations_Force "Lithuanian Special Operations Force") and other units: Logistics Command, Training and Doctrine Command, Headquarters Battalion, [Military Police](/wiki/Lithuanian_Military_Police "Lithuanian Military Police"). Directly subordinated to the [Chief of Defence](/wiki/Chief_of_Defence "Chief of Defence") are the [Special Operations Forces](/wiki/Special_Operations_Forces "Special Operations Forces") and Military Police. The Reserve Forces are under command of the [Lithuanian National Defence Volunteer Forces](/wiki/Lithuanian_National_Defence_Volunteer_Forces "Lithuanian National Defence Volunteer Forces").

The Lithuanian Armed Forces consist of some 20,000 active personnel, which may be supported by [reserve forces](/wiki/Reserve_forces "Reserve forces").[[176]](#cite_note-180) Compulsory [conscription](/wiki/Conscription "Conscription") ended in 2008 but was reintroduced in 2015.[[177]](#cite_note-181) The Lithuanian Armed Forces currently have 30 soldiers and officers participating in nine international operations and European Union training missions deployed in [Kosovo](/wiki/Kosovo "Kosovo"), [Iraq](/wiki/Iraq "Iraq"), [Central African Republic](/wiki/Central_African_Republic "Central African Republic"), [Djibouti](/wiki/Djibouti "Djibouti"), [Mozambique](/wiki/Mozambique "Mozambique"), [Spain](/wiki/Spain "Spain"), [Italy](/wiki/Italy "Italy"), and in the [United Kingdom](/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom"), providing training for Ukrainian soldiers on [Operation Interflex](/wiki/Operation_Interflex "Operation Interflex").[[178]](#cite_note-182)[[179]](#cite_note-183)

Lithuania became a full member of [NATO](/wiki/NATO "NATO") in March 2004. [Fighter jets](/wiki/Fighter_jets "Fighter jets") of NATO members are deployed in [Šiauliai Air Base](/wiki/%C5%A0iauliai_Air_Base "Šiauliai Air Base") and [provide safety for the Baltic airspace](/wiki/Baltic_Air_Policing "Baltic Air Policing").

Beginning in summer of 2005, Lithuania was part of the [International Security Assistance Force](/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force "International Security Assistance Force") in Afghanistan (ISAF), leading a [Provincial Reconstruction Team](/wiki/Provincial_Reconstruction_Team "Provincial Reconstruction Team") (PRT) in the town of [Chaghcharan](/wiki/Chaghcharan "Chaghcharan") in the province of [Ghor](/wiki/Ghor "Ghor"). The PRT included personnel from [Denmark](/wiki/Denmark "Denmark"), Iceland and the [US](/wiki/US "US"). There were also [special operation forces](/wiki/Lithuanian_Special_Operations_Force "Lithuanian Special Operations Force") units in Afghanistan, placed in [Kandahar Province](/wiki/Kandahar_Province "Kandahar Province"). Since joining international operations in 1994, Lithuania has lost two soldiers: Lt. Normundas Valteris fell in [Bosnia](/wiki/Bosnia "Bosnia"), as his patrol vehicle drove over a mine. Sgt. Arūnas Jarmalavičius was fatally wounded during an attack on the camp of his Provincial Reconstruction Team in [Afghanistan](/wiki/Afghanistan "Afghanistan").[[180]](#cite_note-184)

The Lithuanian National Defence Policy aims to guarantee the preservation of the [independence](/wiki/Independence "Independence") and [sovereignty](/wiki/Sovereignty "Sovereignty") of the state, the [integrity](/wiki/Integrity "Integrity") of its land, territorial waters and airspace, and its constitutional order. Its main strategic goals are to defend the country's interests, and to maintain and expand the capabilities of its armed forces so they may contribute to and participate in the missions of NATO and European Union member states.[[181]](#cite_note-WB-185)

The [defense ministry](/wiki/Ministry_of_National_Defence_(Lithuania) "Ministry of National Defence (Lithuania)") is responsible for combat forces, [search and rescue](/wiki/Search_and_rescue "Search and rescue"), and intelligence operations. The 5,000 [border guards](/wiki/State_Border_Guard_Service_(Lithuania) "State Border Guard Service (Lithuania)") fall under the [Interior Ministry](/wiki/Interior_Ministry "Interior Ministry")'s supervision and are responsible for border protection, passport and customs duties, and share responsibility with the [navy](/wiki/Lithuanian_Naval_Force "Lithuanian Naval Force") for [smuggling](/wiki/Smuggling "Smuggling") and drug trafficking interdiction. A special security department handles VIP protection and [communications security](/wiki/Communications_security "Communications security"). In 2015 [National Cyber Security Centre of Lithuania](/wiki/National_Cyber_Security_Centre_of_Lithuania "National Cyber Security Centre of Lithuania") was created. Paramilitary organisation [Lithuanian Riflemen's Union](/wiki/Lithuanian_Riflemen%27s_Union "Lithuanian Riflemen's Union") acts as a civilian self-defence institution.

According to NATO, in 2020, Lithuania allocated 2.13% of its [GDP](/wiki/Gross_domestic_product "Gross domestic product") to the national defense.[[182]](#cite_note-nato-lt-spending-186) For a long time, especially after the [global financial crisis](/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008 "Financial crisis of 2007–2008") in 2008, Lithuania lagged behind NATO allies in terms of defence spending. However, in recent years it has begun to rapidly increase the funding, exceeding the NATO guideline of 2% in 2019.

Lithuania's president [Gitanas Nausėda](/wiki/Gitanas_Naus%C4%97da "Gitanas Nausėda") called for more NATO troops on 22 April 2022, saying NATO should increase its deployment of troops in Lithuania and elsewhere on Europe's eastern flank following [Russia's invasion of Ukraine](/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine"), during a meeting in Vilnius.[[183]](#cite_note-187)

Economy
-------

Main article: [Economy of Lithuania](/wiki/Economy_of_Lithuania "Economy of Lithuania")

See also: [List of companies of Lithuania](/wiki/List_of_companies_of_Lithuania "List of companies of Lithuania")

|  |  |  |  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  | **This article has multiple issues.** Please help **[improve it](/wiki/Special:EditPage/Lithuania "Special:EditPage/Lithuania")** or discuss these issues on the **[talk page](/wiki/Talk:Lithuania "Talk:Lithuania")**. *([Learn how and when to remove these messages](/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal "Help:Maintenance template removal"))* |  |  | | --- | --- | |  | This section needs to be **updated**. Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information. *(May 2024)* |  |  |  | | --- | --- | |  | This section **needs expansion**. You can help by [making an edit request](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Talk%3ALithuania&preload=Template%3ASubmit+an+edit+request%2Fpreload&action=edit&section=new&editintro=Template%3AEdit+protected%2Feditintro&preloadtitle=Protected+edit+request+on+14+March+2025&preloadparams%5B%5D=edit+fully-protected&preloadparams%5B%5D=Lithuania)[adding to it](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lithuania&action=edit&section=) . *(January 2025)* |  *([Learn how and when to remove this message](/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal "Help:Maintenance template removal"))* |

Lithuania has an open and [mixed economy](/wiki/Mixed_economy "Mixed economy") that is classified as a [high-income economy](/wiki/High-income_economy "High-income economy") by the [World Bank](/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank").[[185]](#cite_note-189) As of 2017[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lithuania&action=edit) the three largest sectors were – services (67% of GDP), industry (29%) and agriculture (3%).[[186]](#cite_note-190) Lithuania joined [NATO](/wiki/NATO "NATO") in 2004,[[187]](#cite_note-191) EU in 2004,[[188]](#cite_note-192) [Schengen](/wiki/Schengen_Area "Schengen Area") in 2007[[189]](#cite_note-193) and [OECD](/wiki/OECD "OECD") in 2018.[[151]](#cite_note-Lithuania's_accession_to_the_OECD-155) On 1 January 2015, the euro became the national currency, replacing [litas](/wiki/Litas "Litas") at the rate of EUR 1.00 = LTL 3.45280.[[190]](#cite_note-iso4217news2014-194)

Agricultural products and food comprise 18% of exports; other major sectors include chemical products and plastics (18%), machinery and appliances (16%), mineral products (15%), wood and furniture (13%).[[191]](#cite_note-195) As of 2016[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lithuania&action=edit) more than half of exports go to 7 countries including Russia (14%), Latvia (10%), Poland (9%), Germany (8%), Estonia (5%), Sweden (%) and the UK (4%).[[192]](#cite_note-196) Exports equaled 81% of GDP in 2017.[[193]](#cite_note-197)

GDP experienced very high real growth rates for the decade up to 2009, peaking at 11% in 2007. As a result, the country was often termed a [Baltic Tiger](/wiki/Baltic_Tiger "Baltic Tiger"). However, in 2009 due to the [2007–2008 financial crisis](/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_financial_crisis "2007–2008 financial crisis"), GDP contracted 15%[[194]](#cite_note-198) and unemployment rate reached 17.8% in 2010.[[195]](#cite_note-199) Growth has since been much slower. According to the IMF, financial conditions are conducive to growth and financial soundness indicators remain strong. The public debt ratio in 2016 was 40% of GDP, it had been 15% in 2008.[[196]](#cite_note-200)

On average, more than 95% of all [foreign direct investment](/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment "Foreign direct investment") comes from EU countries. Sweden is historically the largest investor with 20% – 30% of FDI.[[197]](#cite_note-201) FDI into Lithuania spiked in 2017, reaching its highest ever recorded number of greenfield investment projects. In 2017, Lithuania was third, after [Ireland](/wiki/Ireland "Ireland") and [Singapore](/wiki/Singapore "Singapore") by the average job value of investment projects.[[198]](#cite_note-202)
The US was the leading source country in 2017, 25% of total FDI. Next up were Germany and the UK, each representing 11% of total project numbers.[[199]](#cite_note-203) Based on the Eurostat's data, in 2017, the value of exports recorded the most rapid growth not only in the Baltic countries, but across Europe, which was 17%.[[200]](#cite_note-204)

Between 2004 and 2016, one out of five Lithuanians emigrated, primarily due to insufficient income for residents;[[201]](#cite_note-205) secondarily seeking to study. Long term emigration and economic growth has resulted in a shortage in the labor market[[202]](#cite_note-206) and growth in salaries being larger than growth in labor efficiency.[[203]](#cite_note-archive_org_11-207) Unemployment in 2017 was 8%.[[204]](#cite_note-208)

As of 2022, Lithuanian [median](/wiki/Median "Median") wealth per adult was [$](/wiki/USD "USD")32,000 ([mean](/wiki/Mean "Mean") was $70,000), while total national wealth was $147bn.[[205]](#cite_note-CS2023-1-209) As of 2023 Q2, the average monthly gross salary in Lithuania was €2,000.[[206]](#cite_note-https://osp.stat.gov.lt-210)

Lithuania has a [flat tax](/wiki/Flat_tax "Flat tax") rather than a [progressive scheme](/wiki/Progressive_tax "Progressive tax"). The personal income tax (15%) and corporate tax (15%) rates are among the lowest in the EU.[[207]](#cite_note-epp.eurostat.ec.europa.eu-211) The country has the lowest implicit rate of tax on capital (9.8%) in the EU. The corporate tax rate is 15% and 5% for small businesses; 7 [free economic zones](/wiki/Free_economic_zone "Free economic zone") operate.[[208]](#cite_note-212)

[Information technology](/wiki/Information_technology "Information technology") production is growing, reaching €2 billion in 2016.[[209]](#cite_note-213) In 2017 only, 35[[210]](#cite_note-214) [FinTech](/wiki/FinTech "FinTech") companies came to Lithuania – a result of the government and Bank of Lithuania simplifying procedures.[[211]](#cite_note-215) Lithuania has granted a total of 39 e-money licenses, second in the EU only to the UK with 128 licenses. In 2018 [Google](/wiki/Google "Google") set up a payment company in Lithuania.[[212]](#cite_note-216) Europe's first international Blockchain Centre launched in Vilnius in 2018.[[213]](#cite_note-217) Since 2021, Lithuania has issued hundreds of licenses for cryptocurrency exchange and storage operations, making it one of the leading countries in the EU in this sector.[[214]](#cite_note-218)

### Agriculture

Main article: [Agriculture in Lithuania](/wiki/Agriculture_in_Lithuania "Agriculture in Lithuania")

Agriculture in Lithuania dates to the [Neolithic](/wiki/Neolithic "Neolithic") period, about 3,000 to 1,000 [BC](/wiki/Before_Christ "Before Christ"). It has been one of Lithuania's most important occupations for many centuries.[[215]](#cite_note-219) Lithuania's accession to the [European Union](/wiki/European_Union "European Union") in 2004 ushered in a new agricultural era. The EU pursues a very high standard of food safety and purity. In 1999, the [Seimas](/wiki/Seimas "Seimas") (parliament) of Lithuania adopted a Law on Product Safety, and in 2000 it adopted a Law on Food.[[216]](#cite_note-220)[[217]](#cite_note-221) The reform of the agricultural market has been carried out on the basis of these two laws.

In 2016, agricultural production was €2.3 billion. [Cereal crops](/wiki/Cereal_crops "Cereal crops") occupied the largest part (5710 tons), other significant types include: [sugar beet](/wiki/Sugar_beet "Sugar beet") (934 tons), [rapeseed](/wiki/Rapeseed "Rapeseed") (393 tons) and [potatoes](/wiki/Potato "Potato") (340 tons). Products totaling €4,385 million were exported to foreign markets, of which products for €3,165 million were of Lithuanian origin. Export of agricultural and food products accounted for 19% of all exports of goods.[[218]](#cite_note-222)

[Organic farming](/wiki/Organic_farming "Organic farming") is becoming more popular. The status of organic growers and producers is granted by the public body *Ekoagros*. In 2016, there were 2539 such farms that occupied 225,542 hectares. Of these, 43% were cereals, 31% perennial grasses, 14% leguminous crops and 12% others.[[219]](#cite_note-223)

### Science and technology

Lithuanian nobleman and [artillery](/wiki/Artillery "Artillery") expert [Kazimieras Simonavičius](/wiki/Kazimieras_Simonavi%C4%8Dius "Kazimieras Simonavičius") developed and popularized the concept of a [multistage rocket](/wiki/Multistage_rocket "Multistage rocket").

The foundation of the [University of Vilnius](/wiki/University_of_Vilnius "University of Vilnius") in 1579 was a major factor in fostering a scientific and academic community within Lithuania. The university has welcomed such prominent scientists and thinkers as [Georg Forster](/wiki/Georg_Forster "Georg Forster"), [Jean-Emmanuel Gilibert](/wiki/Jean-Emmanuel_Gilibert "Jean-Emmanuel Gilibert"), [Johann Peter Frank](/wiki/Johann_Peter_Frank "Johann Peter Frank"). The 17th century artillery expert [Kazimieras Simonavičius](/wiki/Kazimieras_Simonavi%C4%8Dius "Kazimieras Simonavičius") is considered a pioneer of [rocketry](/wiki/Rocket "Rocket"); his publication, the *Artis Magnae Artilleriae,* was a basic [artillery](/wiki/Artillery "Artillery") manual throughout Europe, containing a large chapter on caliber, construction, production and properties of rockets (for military and civil purposes), including [multistage rockets](/wiki/Multistage_rocket "Multistage rocket"), [batteries of rockets](/wiki/Rocket_battery "Rocket battery"), and rockets with [delta wing](/wiki/Delta_wing "Delta wing") [stabilizers](/wiki/Stabilizer_(aircraft) "Stabilizer (aircraft)").[[220]](#cite_note-224)[[221]](#cite_note-225) Botanist [Jurgis Pabrėža](/wiki/Jurgis_Pabr%C4%97%C5%BEa "Jurgis Pabrėža") (1771–1849) created the first systematic guide of Lithuanian flora, *Taislius auguminis* (*Botany*), written in the Samogitian dialect, the Latin-Lithuanian dictionary of plant names, and the first Lithuanian geography textbook. German scientist [Theodor Grotthuss](/wiki/Theodor_Grotthuss "Theodor Grotthuss") (1785–1822), who proposed the [Grotthuss mechanism](/wiki/Grotthuss_mechanism "Grotthuss mechanism"), lived and worked in the [Gedučiai manor](/w/index.php?title=Gedu%C4%8Diai_manor&action=edit&redlink=1 "Gedučiai manor (page does not exist)") [[lt](https://lt.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gedu%C4%8Di%C5%B3_dvaras "lt:Gedučių dvaras")], where he gained local prominence for his effort to educate and improve the well-being of peasants.[[222]](#cite_note-226)

The world wars of the 20th century severely diminished Lithuanian science and academia, although Lithuanian scholars and scientists managed to succeed, particularly abroad, including philosopher [Vosylius Sezemanas](/wiki/Vasily_Seseman "Vasily Seseman"), jurist [Mykolas Römeris](/wiki/Micha%C5%82_Pius_R%C3%B6mer "Michał Pius Römer"), aviator [Antanas Gustaitis](/wiki/Antanas_Gustaitis "Antanas Gustaitis"), management theorist [Vytautas Andrius Graičiūnas](/wiki/Vytautas_Andrius_Grai%C4%8Di%C5%ABnas "Vytautas Andrius Graičiūnas"), archaeologist [Marija Gimbutas](/wiki/Marija_Gimbutas "Marija Gimbutas"), primatologist [Birutė Galdikas](/wiki/Birut%C4%97_Galdikas "Birutė Galdikas"), linguist [Algirdas Julien Greimas](/wiki/Algirdas_Julien_Greimas "Algirdas Julien Greimas"), and [medievalist](/wiki/Medieval_studies "Medieval studies") [Jurgis Baltrušaitis](/wiki/Jurgis_Baltru%C5%A1aitis_(son) "Jurgis Baltrušaitis (son)").[[223]](#cite_note-227)[[224]](#cite_note-228)[[225]](#cite_note-229)[[226]](#cite_note-230)[[227]](#cite_note-231) Mathematician [Jonas Kubilius](/wiki/Jonas_Kubilius "Jonas Kubilius"), long-term rector of the [University of Vilnius](/wiki/University_of_Vilnius "University of Vilnius"), is known for works in [Probabilistic number theory](/wiki/Probabilistic_number_theory "Probabilistic number theory"), including the [Kubilius model](/wiki/Kubilius_model "Kubilius model"), Theorem of Kubilius and the [Turán–Kubilius inequality](/wiki/Tur%C3%A1n%E2%80%93Kubilius_inequality "Turán–Kubilius inequality"). Kubilius also successfully resisted attempts to Russify the university.[[228]](#cite_note-232)

[Lasers](/wiki/Laser "Laser") and [biotechnology](/wiki/Biotechnology "Biotechnology") are flagship fields of the Lithuanian science and high-tech industry.[[229]](#cite_note-233)[[230]](#cite_note-234) *Šviesos konversija* ("Light Conversion") has developed a [femtosecond](/wiki/Femtosecond "Femtosecond") laser system that has 80% market share worldwide, with applications in DNA research, ophthalmological surgeries, and nanotechnology.[[231]](#cite_note-235)[[232]](#cite_note-236) The [Vilnius University](/wiki/Vilnius_University "Vilnius University") Laser Research Center has developed one of the most powerful femtosecond lasers in the world dedicated primarily to [oncological diseases](/wiki/Oncology "Oncology").[[233]](#cite_note-237) In 1963, [Vytautas Straižys](/wiki/Vytautas_Strai%C5%BEys "Vytautas Straižys") and his colleagues created [Vilnius photometric system](/wiki/Vilnius_photometric_system "Vilnius photometric system") that is used in [astronomy](/wiki/Astronomy "Astronomy").[[234]](#cite_note-238) Noninvasive intracranial pressure and blood flow measuring devices were developed by [KTU](/wiki/Kaunas_University_of_Technology "Kaunas University of Technology") scientist A. Ragauskas.[[235]](#cite_note-239) Kęstutis Pyragas contributed to the study of [chaos theory](/wiki/Control_of_chaos "Control of chaos") with his method of delayed feedback control, the [Pyragas method](/wiki/Pyragas_method "Pyragas method"). [Kavli Prize](/wiki/Kavli_Prize "Kavli Prize") laureate [Virginijus Šikšnys](/wiki/Virginijus_%C5%A0ik%C5%A1nys "Virginijus Šikšnys") is known for his discoveries in [CRISPR](/wiki/CRISPR "CRISPR"), namely with respect to CRISPR-[Cas9](/wiki/Cas9 "Cas9").[[236]](#cite_note-240)[[237]](#cite_note-241)

Lithuania has launched three satellites to space: [LitSat-1](/wiki/LitSat-1 "LitSat-1"), [Lituanica SAT-1](/wiki/Lituanica_SAT-1 "Lituanica SAT-1") and [LituanicaSAT-2](/wiki/LituanicaSAT-2 "LituanicaSAT-2").[[238]](#cite_note-242) [Lithuanian Museum of Ethnocosmology](/wiki/Lithuanian_Museum_of_Ethnocosmology "Lithuanian Museum of Ethnocosmology") and [Molėtai Astronomical Observatory](/wiki/Mol%C4%97tai_Astronomical_Observatory "Molėtai Astronomical Observatory") is located in [Kulionys](/wiki/Kulionys "Kulionys").[[239]](#cite_note-243) Fifteen R&D institutions are members of [Lithuanian Space Association](/wiki/Lithuanian_Space_Association "Lithuanian Space Association"); Lithuania is a cooperating state with [European Space Agency](/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency").[[240]](#cite_note-244)[[241]](#cite_note-245) [Rimantas Stankevičius](/wiki/Rimantas_Stankevi%C4%8Dius "Rimantas Stankevičius") is the only ethnically Lithuanian [astronaut](/wiki/Astronaut "Astronaut").[[242]](#cite_note-246)

Lithuania in 2018 became an Associated Member State of [CERN](/wiki/CERN "CERN").[[243]](#cite_note-247) Two CERN incubators in Vilnius and Kaunas will be hosted.[[244]](#cite_note-248) The most advanced scientific research is being conducted at the Life Sciences Center,[[245]](#cite_note-249) Center For Physical Sciences and Technology.[[246]](#cite_note-250)

As of 2016 calculations, yearly growth of Lithuania's biotech and life science sector was 22% over the past 5 years. 16 academic institutions, 15 R&D centres (science parks and innovation valleys) and more than 370 manufacturers operate in the Lithuanian life science and biotech industry.[[247]](#cite_note-251)

In 2008 the Valley development programme was started aiming to upgrade Lithuanian scientific research infrastructure and encourage business and science cooperation. Five R&D Valleys were launched – Jūrinis (maritime technologies), Nemunas (agro, bioenergy, forestry), Saulėtekis (laser and light, semiconductor), Santara (biotechnology, medicine), Santaka (sustainable chemistry and pharmacy).[[248]](#cite_note-252) Lithuanian Innovation Center is created to provide support for innovations and research institutions.[[249]](#cite_note-253)

Lithuania ranks moderately in the [International Innovation Index](/wiki/International_Innovation_Index "International Innovation Index"),[[250]](#cite_note-254) and is placed 15th among EU countries by the [European Innovation Scoreboard](/wiki/European_Innovation_Scoreboard "European Innovation Scoreboard").[[251]](#cite_note-255) Lithuania was ranked 35th in the [Global Innovation Index](/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index "Global Innovation Index") in 2024.[[252]](#cite_note-256)

### Tourism

Main article: [Tourism in Lithuania](/wiki/Tourism_in_Lithuania "Tourism in Lithuania")

Statistics from 2023 showed 1.4 million tourists from foreign countries visited Lithuania and spent at least one night. The largest number of tourists came from [Poland](/wiki/Poland "Poland") (173,500), [Latvia](/wiki/Latvia "Latvia") (144,300), [Belarus](/wiki/Belarus "Belarus") (141,900), [Germany](/wiki/Germany "Germany") (127,400), the [United Kingdom](/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom") (74,200), the [United States](/wiki/United_States "United States") (69,700), [Ukraine](/wiki/Ukraine "Ukraine") (67,000), and [Estonia](/wiki/Estonia "Estonia") (61,300).[[253]](#cite_note-tou-257)

Domestic tourism has been on the rise as well. Currently there are up to 1000 places of attraction in Lithuania. Most tourists visit the big cities—[Vilnius](/wiki/Vilnius "Vilnius"), [Klaipėda](/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da "Klaipėda"), and [Kaunas](/wiki/Kaunas "Kaunas"), seaside resorts, such as [Neringa](/wiki/Neringa_Municipality "Neringa Municipality"), [Palanga](/wiki/Palanga "Palanga"), and [Spa towns](/wiki/Spa_town "Spa town") – [Druskininkai](/wiki/Druskininkai "Druskininkai"), [Birštonas](/wiki/Bir%C5%A1tonas "Birštonas").[[254]](#cite_note-258)

Hot air ballooning is popular, especially in Vilnius and Trakai. Bicycle tourism is growing, especially the Lithuanian Seaside Cycle Route. [EuroVelo](/wiki/EuroVelo "EuroVelo") routes EV10, EV11, EV13 go through Lithuania. The total length of bicycle tracks amounts to 3769 km (of which 1988 km is asphalt pavement).[[255]](#cite_note-259) [Nemunas Delta Regional Park](/wiki/Nemunas_Delta_Regional_Park "Nemunas Delta Regional Park") and [Žuvintas biosphere reserve](/wiki/%C5%BDuvintas "Žuvintas") are known for birdwatching.[[256]](#cite_note-260)

The total contribution of tourism to GDP had been forecast to rise to €3.2 billion, 7% of GDP by 2027,[[257]](#cite_note-261) but has decreased to €1.7 billion, 2.3% of GDP in 2023, although it is rising post [COVID-19 pandemic](/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic "COVID-19 pandemic").[[258]](#cite_note-MCTo-262)

Infrastructure
--------------

### Communication

Main article: [Telecommunications in Lithuania](/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Lithuania "Telecommunications in Lithuania")

Lithuania has a well developed communications infrastructure. The country has 2.8 million citizens[[259]](#cite_note-263) and 5 million SIM cards.[[260]](#cite_note-264) The largest LTE (4G) mobile network covers 97% of Lithuania's territory.[[261]](#cite_note-265) Usage of fixed phone lines has been rapidly decreasing due to rapid expansion of mobile-cellular services.[[262]](#cite_note-266)

In 2017, Lithuania was top 30 in the world by average mobile broadband speeds and top 20 by average fixed broadband speeds.[[263]](#cite_note-267)
Lithuania was also top 7 in 2017 in the [List of countries by 4G LTE penetration](/wiki/List_of_countries_by_4G_LTE_penetration "List of countries by 4G LTE penetration"). In 2016, Lithuania was ranked 17th in United Nations' e-participation index.[[264]](#cite_note-268)[[265]](#cite_note-269)

There are four TIER III datacenters in Lithuania.[[266]](#cite_note-270)
Lithuania is 44th globally ranked country on data center density according to Cloudscene.[[267]](#cite_note-271)

Long-term project (2005–2013) – Development of Rural Areas Broadband Network (RAIN) was started with the objective to provide residents, state and municipal authorities and businesses with fibre-optic broadband access in rural areas. RAIN infrastructure allows 51 communications operators to provide network services to their clients. The project was funded by the European Union and the Lithuanian government.[[268]](#cite_note-272)[[269]](#cite_note-273) 72% of Lithuanian households have access to internet, a number which in 2017 was among EU's lowest[[270]](#cite_note-274) and in 2016 ranked 97th by [CIA World Factbook](/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook "CIA World Factbook").[[271]](#cite_note-275) Number of households with internet access is expected to increase and reach 77% by 2021.[[272]](#cite_note-276) Almost 50% of Lithuanians had smartphones in 2016, a number that is expected to increase to 65% by 2022.[[273]](#cite_note-277)
Lithuania has the highest FTTH (Fiber to the home) penetration rate in Europe (36.8% in September 2016) according to FTTH Council Europe.[[274]](#cite_note-278)

### Transport

Main article: [Transport in Lithuania](/wiki/Transport_in_Lithuania "Transport in Lithuania")

Lithuania received its first railway connection in the middle of the 19th century, when the [Warsaw – Saint Petersburg Railway](/wiki/Warsaw_%E2%80%93_Saint_Petersburg_Railway "Warsaw – Saint Petersburg Railway") was constructed. It included a stretch from [Daugavpils](/wiki/Daugavpils "Daugavpils") via Vilnius and Kaunas to Virbalis. The first and only still operating [tunnel](/wiki/Kaunas_Railway_Tunnel "Kaunas Railway Tunnel") was completed in 1860.

[Rail transport in Lithuania](/wiki/Rail_transport_in_Lithuania "Rail transport in Lithuania") consists of 1,762 km (1,095 mi) of 1,520 mm (4 ft 11.8 in) [Russian gauge](/wiki/Russian_gauge "Russian gauge") railway of which 122 km (76 mi) are electrified. This railway network is incompatible with European [standard gauge](/wiki/Standard_gauge "Standard gauge") and requires train switching. However, Lithuanian railway network also has 115 km (71 mi) of [standard gauge](/wiki/Standard_gauge "Standard gauge") lines.[[275]](#cite_note-279) More than half of all inland freight transported in Lithuania is carried by rail.[[276]](#cite_note-280) The Trans-European standard gauge [Rail Baltica](/wiki/Rail_Baltica "Rail Baltica") railway, linking [Helsinki](/wiki/Helsinki "Helsinki")–[Tallinn](/wiki/Tallinn "Tallinn")–[Riga](/wiki/Riga "Riga")–[Kaunas](/wiki/Kaunas "Kaunas")–[Warsaw](/wiki/Warsaw "Warsaw") and continuing on to Berlin is under construction. In 2017, [Lietuvos Geležinkeliai](/wiki/Lietuvos_Gele%C5%BEinkeliai "Lietuvos Geležinkeliai"), a company that operates most railway lines in Lithuania, received EU penalty for breaching EU's antitrust laws and restricting competition.[[277]](#cite_note-281)

Transportation is the third largest sector in Lithuanian economy.[[278]](#cite_note-282) Lithuanian transport companies drew attention in 2016[[279]](#cite_note-283) and 2017[[280]](#cite_note-284) with huge and record-breaking orders of trucks. Almost 90% of commercial truck traffic in Lithuania is international transports, the highest of any EU country.[[281]](#cite_note-285)

Lithuania has an extensive network of motorways. WEF grades Lithuanian roads at 4.7 / 7.0[[282]](#cite_note-286) and Lithuanian road authority (LAKD) at 6.5 / 10.0.[[283]](#cite_note-287)

The [Port of Klaipėda](/wiki/Port_of_Klaip%C4%97da "Port of Klaipėda") is the only commercial cargo port in Lithuania. In 2011 45.5 million tons of cargo were handled (including [Būtingė oil terminal](/wiki/B%C5%ABting%C4%97_oil_terminal "Būtingė oil terminal") figures)[[284]](#cite_note-288) [Port of Klaipėda](/wiki/Port_of_Klaip%C4%97da "Port of Klaipėda") is outside of EU's 20 largest ports,[[285]](#cite_note-289)[[286]](#cite_note-290) but it is the eighth largest port in the [Baltic Sea](/wiki/Baltic_Sea "Baltic Sea") region[[287]](#cite_note-291)[[288]](#cite_note-292) with ongoing expansion plans.[[289]](#cite_note-293)

As of 2022, the LIWA (Lithuanian Inland Waterways Authority, Vidaus vandens keliu direkcija in Lithuanian) is developing a strategy to resurrect cargo shipping on the Nemunas. Its fleet of electric ships will travel 260 km between the port of Klaipda on the Baltic Sea coast and the industrial and transportation centre of [Kaunas](/wiki/Kaunas "Kaunas").[[290]](#cite_note-:159-294) The project is anticipated to need a €75.7 million initial investment in total. and estimated to eliminate 48 000 truck trips annually.[[291]](#cite_note-295)[[292]](#cite_note-296)

[Vilnius International Airport](/wiki/Vilnius_International_Airport "Vilnius International Airport") is the largest airport in Lithuania, 91st busiest airport in Europe ([EU's 100 largest airports](/wiki/List_of_the_busiest_airports_in_Europe "List of the busiest airports in Europe")). It served 3.8 million passengers in 2016.[[293]](#cite_note-297) Other international airports include [Kaunas International Airport](/wiki/Kaunas_Airport "Kaunas Airport"), [Palanga International Airport](/wiki/Palanga_International_Airport "Palanga International Airport") and [Šiauliai International Airport](/wiki/%C5%A0iauliai_International_Airport "Šiauliai International Airport"). [Kaunas International Airport](/wiki/Kaunas_Airport "Kaunas Airport") is also a small commercial cargo airport which started regular commercial cargo traffic in 2011.[[294]](#cite_note-298) The inland river cargo port in Marvelė, linking Kaunas and Klaipėda, received first cargo in 2019.[[295]](#cite_note-299)

### Energy

Main article: [Energy in Lithuania](/wiki/Energy_in_Lithuania "Energy in Lithuania")

See also: [Renewable energy in Lithuania](/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Lithuania "Renewable energy in Lithuania")

Systematic diversification of energy imports and resources is Lithuania's key energy strategy.[[296]](#cite_note-300) Long-term aims were defined in National Energy Independence strategy in 2012 by Lietuvos Seimas.[[297]](#cite_note-301) It was estimated that strategic energy independence initiatives will cost €6.3–7.8 billion in total and provide annual savings of €0.9–1.1 billion.

After the decommissioning of the [Ignalina Nuclear Power Plant](/wiki/Ignalina_Nuclear_Power_Plant "Ignalina Nuclear Power Plant"), Lithuania turned from electricity exporter to electricity importer. Unit No. 1 was closed in December 2004, as a condition of Lithuania's entry into the European Union; Unit No. 2 was closed down on 31 December 2009. Proposals have been made to construct a new – [Visaginas Nuclear Power Plant](/wiki/Visaginas_Nuclear_Power_Plant "Visaginas Nuclear Power Plant") in Lithuania.[[298]](#cite_note-302) However, [a non-binding referendum](/wiki/2012_Lithuanian_nuclear_power_referendum "2012 Lithuanian nuclear power referendum") held in October 2012 clouded the prospects for the Visaginas project, as 63% of voters said no to a new nuclear power plant.[[299]](#cite_note-303)

The country's main primary source of electrical power is [Elektrėnai Power Plant](/wiki/Elektr%C4%97nai_Power_Plant "Elektrėnai Power Plant"). Other primary sources of Lithuania's electrical power are [Kruonis Pumped Storage Plant](/wiki/Kruonis_Pumped_Storage_Plant "Kruonis Pumped Storage Plant") and [Kaunas Hydroelectric Power Plant](/wiki/Kaunas_Hydroelectric_Power_Plant "Kaunas Hydroelectric Power Plant"). [Kruonis Pumped Storage Plant](/wiki/Kruonis_Pumped_Storage_Plant "Kruonis Pumped Storage Plant") is the only in the Baltic states power plant to be used for regulation of the power system's operation with generating capacity of 900 MW for at least 12 hours.[[300]](#cite_note-304) As of 2015[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lithuania&action=edit), 66% of electrical power was imported.[[301]](#cite_note-305) First geothermal heating plant ([Klaipėda Geothermal Demonstration Plant](/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da_Geothermal_Demonstration_Plant "Klaipėda Geothermal Demonstration Plant")) in the Baltic Sea region was built in 2004.

Lithuania–Sweden submarine electricity interconnection [NordBalt](/wiki/NordBalt "NordBalt") and Lithuania–Poland electricity interconnection [LitPol Link](/wiki/LitPol_Link "LitPol Link") were launched at the end of 2015.[[302]](#cite_note-306)

In 2018, synchronising the Baltic states' electricity grid with the [Synchronous grid of Continental Europe](/wiki/Synchronous_grid_of_Continental_Europe "Synchronous grid of Continental Europe") has started.[[303]](#cite_note-307) In 2016, 20.8% of electricity consumed in Lithuania came from renewable sources.[[304]](#cite_note-308)

In order to break down [Gazprom](/wiki/Gazprom "Gazprom")'s monopoly[[305]](#cite_note-309)[[306]](#cite_note-310) in natural gas market of Lithuania, first large scale LNG import terminal ([Klaipėda LNG FSRU](/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da_LNG_FSRU "Klaipėda LNG FSRU")) in the Baltic region was built in port of Klaipėda in 2014. The Klaipėda LNG terminal was called Independence, thus emphasising the aim to diversify energy market of Lithuania. Norwegian company [Equinor](/wiki/Equinor "Equinor") supplies 540 million cubic metres (19 billion cubic feet) of natural gas annually from 2015 until 2020.[[307]](#cite_note-311) The terminal is able to meet the Lithuania's demand 100 percent, and Latvia's and Estonia's national demand 90 percent in the future.[[308]](#cite_note-312) [Gas Interconnection Poland–Lithuania](/wiki/Gas_Interconnection_Poland%E2%80%93Lithuania "Gas Interconnection Poland–Lithuania") (GIPL), also known as Lithuania–Poland pipeline, is a natural gas pipeline interconnection between Lithuania and Poland that became operational in 2022.

Demographics
------------

Main article: [Demographics of Lithuania](/wiki/Demographics_of_Lithuania "Demographics of Lithuania")

Since the Neolithic period, the demographics of Lithuania have stayed fairly homogenous. There is a high probability that the inhabitants of present-day Lithuania have similar genetic compositions to their ancestors,[[309]](#cite_note-313)[[310]](#cite_note-314)[[311]](#cite_note-315) although without being actually isolated from them.[[312]](#cite_note-316) The Lithuanian population appears to be relatively homogeneous, without apparent genetic differences among ethnic subgroups.[[313]](#cite_note-317)

A 2004 analysis of [MtDNA](/wiki/MtDNA "MtDNA") in the Lithuanian population revealed that Lithuanians are genetically close to the [Slavic](/wiki/Slavic_languages "Slavic languages") and [Finno-Ugric](/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages "Finno-Ugric languages") speaking populations of Northern and Eastern Europe. [Y-chromosome](/wiki/Y_chromosome "Y chromosome") SNP [haplogroup](/wiki/Haplogroup "Haplogroup") analysis showed Lithuanians to be genetically closest to [Latvians](/wiki/Latvians "Latvians") and [Estonians](/wiki/Estonians "Estonians").[[314]](#cite_note-318)

In 2021, the age structure of the population was as follows:

* 0–14 years, 14.86% (male 214,113/female 203,117)
* 15–64 years: 65.19% (male 896,400/female 934,467)
* 65 years and over: 19.95% (male 195,269/female 365,014).[[315]](#cite_note-population2022-319)

The median age in 2022 was 44 years (male: 41, female: 47).[[315]](#cite_note-population2022-319)

Lithuania has a [sub-replacement fertility rate](/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility_rate "Sub-replacement fertility rate"): the [total fertility rate](/wiki/Total_fertility_rate "Total fertility rate") (TFR) in Lithuania was 1.34 children born per woman in 2021, and the mean age of women at childbirth was 30.3 years. The average age of first childbirth for women was 28.2 years. The [human sex ratio](/wiki/Human_sex_ratio "Human sex ratio") is male leaning for the age categories 15–44, with 1.0352 males for every female.[[315]](#cite_note-population2022-319) As of 2021[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lithuania&action=edit), 25.6% of births were to unmarried women. The mean [age at first marriage](/wiki/Age_at_first_marriage "Age at first marriage") in 2021 was 28.3 years for women and 30.5 years for men.[[315]](#cite_note-population2022-319)

### Functional urban areas

| [Functional urban areas](/wiki/Larger_urban_zone "Larger urban zone")[[316]](#cite_note-FUA,Euro-320) | Population  (2023) |
| --- | --- |
| [Vilnius urban area](/wiki/Vilnius_urban_area "Vilnius urban area") | 747,864 |
| [Kaunas urban area](/wiki/Kaunas_urban_area "Kaunas urban area") | 403,375 |
| [Panevėžys urban area](/wiki/Panev%C4%97%C5%BEys_urban_area "Panevėžys urban area") | 122,860 |

### Ethnic groups and languages

Main articles: [Lithuanians](/wiki/Lithuanians "Lithuanians"), [Ethnic minorities in Lithuania](/wiki/Ethnic_minorities_in_Lithuania "Ethnic minorities in Lithuania"), and [Demographics of Lithuania § Languages](/wiki/Demographics_of_Lithuania#Languages "Demographics of Lithuania")

| Residents of Lithuania by ethnicity (2024)[[317]](#cite_note-321) | | | | |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  | |  |  | |
| Lithuanians | |  | 82.6% | |
| Poles | |  | 6.3% | |
| Russians | |  | 5.0% | |
| Belarusians | |  | 2.1% | |
| Ukrainians | |  | 1.7% | |
| Others | |  | 2.3% | |

Lithuania has the most homogeneous population in the Baltic States. Ethnic Lithuanians make up about five-sixths of the country's population. In 2024, 82.6% of the 2,809,977 Lithuania's residents were ethnic [Lithuanians](/wiki/Lithuanian_people "Lithuanian people") who speak [Lithuanian](/wiki/Lithuanian_language "Lithuanian language"), which is the official language of the country. Several sizeable minorities exist, such as [Poles](/wiki/Polish_people "Polish people") (6.3%), [Russians](/wiki/Russians "Russians") (5.0%), [Belarusians](/wiki/Belarusians "Belarusians") (2.1%) and [Ukrainians](/wiki/Ukrainians "Ukrainians") (1.7%).[[318]](#cite_note-TGKT21-322)

[Poles in Lithuania](/wiki/Poles_in_Lithuania "Poles in Lithuania") are the largest minority, concentrated in southeast Lithuania (the [Vilnius region](/wiki/Vilnius_region "Vilnius region")), constituting majority in [Šalčininkai](/wiki/%C5%A0al%C4%8Dininkai_District_Municipality "Šalčininkai District Municipality") (76.3%) and [Vilnius District Municipality](/wiki/Vilnius_District_Municipality "Vilnius District Municipality") (46.8%). [Russians in Lithuania](/wiki/Russians_in_Lithuania "Russians in Lithuania") are the second largest minority, concentrated in [Visaginas](/wiki/Visaginas_Municipality "Visaginas Municipality") (47.4%), [Zarasai District Municipality](/wiki/Zarasai_District_Municipality "Zarasai District Municipality") (17.2%) and [Klaipėda](/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da "Klaipėda") (16%).[[318]](#cite_note-TGKT21-322) About 2,250 [Roma](/wiki/Romani_people "Romani people") live in Lithuania, mostly in Vilnius, [Kaunas](/wiki/Kaunas "Kaunas") and [Panevėžys](/wiki/Panev%C4%97%C5%BEys "Panevėžys"); their organizations are supported by the National Minority and Emigration Department.[[319]](#cite_note-323) For centuries, [Tatar](/wiki/Lipka_Tatars "Lipka Tatars") and [Karaite](/wiki/Crimean_Karaites "Crimean Karaites") communities have lived in Lithuania. In 2021, there were around 2,150 registered Tatars and 196 Karaites in the country.[[320]](#cite_note-324)[[321]](#cite_note-325)

The official language is [Lithuanian](/wiki/Lithuanian_language "Lithuanian language"), but in some areas there is a significant presence of minority languages such as [Polish](/wiki/Polish_language "Polish language"), [Russian](/wiki/Russian_language "Russian language"), [Belarusian](/wiki/Belarusian_language "Belarusian language") and [Ukrainian](/wiki/Ukrainian_language "Ukrainian language"). The greatest presence of minorities and the use of these languages are in Šalčininkai, Visaginas, and Vilnius District.[[318]](#cite_note-TGKT21-322) In 1941, Lithuania’s Jewish population reached its peak at approximately 250,000 people, making up about 10% of the total population. Today, however, it has dwindled to a very small number. [Yiddish](/wiki/Yiddish "Yiddish") is spoken by members of the tiny remaining [Jewish community](/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Lithuania "History of the Jews in Lithuania") in Lithuania. The state laws guarantee education in minority languages and there are numerous publicly funded schools in the areas populated by minorities, with Polish as the language of instruction being the most widely available.[[322]](#cite_note-326)

According to the survey carried out within the framework of the [Lithuanian census of 2021](/wiki/Lithuanian_census_of_2021 "Lithuanian census of 2021"), 85.33% of the country's population speak Lithuanian as their native language, 6.8% are native speakers of Russian and 5.1% of Polish. As of 2021[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lithuania&action=edit), 60.6% of residents speak Russian as a foreign language, 31.1% – English, 10.5% – Lithuanian, 8% – German, 7.9% – Polish, 1.9% – French, 2.6% – various others.[[323]](#cite_note-327) Most Lithuanian schools teach English as the first foreign language, but students may also study German, or, in some schools, French or Russian. Around 80% of young people in Lithuania know English.[[324]](#cite_note-328)[*[failed verification](/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability "Wikipedia:Verifiability")*]

### Urbanization

See also: [List of cities in Lithuania](/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Lithuania "List of cities in Lithuania"), [List of towns in Lithuania](/wiki/List_of_towns_in_Lithuania "List of towns in Lithuania"), and [List of cities in the Baltic states by population](/wiki/List_of_cities_in_the_Baltic_states_by_population "List of cities in the Baltic states by population")

There has been a steady [movement of population to the cities](/wiki/Urbanization "Urbanization") since the 1990s, encouraged by the planning of regional centres, such as [Alytus](/wiki/Alytus "Alytus"), [Marijampolė](/wiki/Marijampol%C4%97 "Marijampolė"), [Utena](/wiki/Utena "Utena"), [Plungė](/wiki/Plung%C4%97 "Plungė"), and [Mažeikiai](/wiki/Ma%C5%BEeikiai "Mažeikiai"). By the early 21st century, about two-thirds of the total population lived in urban areas. As of 2021[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lithuania&action=edit), 68.19% of the total population lives in urban areas.[[315]](#cite_note-population2022-319) Lithuania's [functional urban areas](/wiki/Larger_urban_zone "Larger urban zone") include [Vilnius](/wiki/Vilnius "Vilnius") (population 708,203), [Kaunas](/wiki/Kaunas "Kaunas") (population 391,153), and [Panevėžys](/wiki/Panev%C4%97%C5%BEys "Panevėžys") (population 124,526).[[316]](#cite_note-FUA,Euro-320) The fDI of the Financial Times in their research *Cities and Regions of the Future* ranked Vilnius fourth in the mid-sized European cities category in the 2018–19 ranking, second in the 2022–23 ranking, second in 2023 ranking while the city claimed 24th spot in the worldwide overall ranking in 2021–22 and Vilnius county was ranked 10th in the small European regions category in 2018–19, fifth in 2022–23, fifth in 2023 rankings.[[325]](#cite_note-329)[[326]](#cite_note-330)[[327]](#cite_note-331)[[328]](#cite_note-332)
